<p><b>Harry S. Truman</b><sup id="cite_ref-initialS_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-initialS-4">[b]</a></sup> (May 8, 1884&#160;– December 26, 1972) was an American politician who served as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">33rd President of the United States</a> (1945–53), assuming that office upon the death of <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> during the waning months of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. He is known for launching the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a> to rebuild the economy of Western Europe, for leading the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> against Soviet and Chinese communism by establishing the <a href="/wiki/Truman_Doctrine" title="Truman Doctrine">Truman Doctrine</a> and <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>, and for intervening in the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>. In domestic affairs, he was a moderate Democrat whose liberal proposals were a continuation of Franklin Roosevelt's <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a>, but the conservative-dominated Congress blocked most of them. He used the veto power 180 times, which is <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_presidential_vetoes" title="List of United States presidential vetoes">more than any president since then</a>, and saw 12 overridden by Congress; only <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> used the veto so often, and only <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">Andrew Johnson</a> saw so many veto overrides.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[3]</a></sup> He also <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">used nuclear weapons</a> to end World War II, <a href="/wiki/Executive_Order_9981" title="Executive Order 9981">desegregated the U.S. armed forces</a>, supported a newly independent <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, and was a founder of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>.</p>
<p>Truman was born in <a href="/wiki/Lamar,_Missouri" title="Lamar, Missouri">Lamar, Missouri</a>, and spent most of his youth on his family's 600-acre farm near <a href="/wiki/Independence,_Missouri" title="Independence, Missouri">Independence</a>. In the last months of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, he served in combat in France as an artillery officer with his <a href="/wiki/Missouri_National_Guard" title="Missouri National Guard">National Guard</a> unit. After the war, he briefly owned a <a href="/wiki/Haberdashery" class="mw-redirect" title="Haberdashery">haberdashery</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City,_Missouri" title="Kansas City, Missouri">Kansas City, Missouri</a>, and joined the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Political_machine" title="Political machine">political machine</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tom_Pendergast" title="Tom Pendergast">Tom Pendergast</a>. Truman was first elected to public office as a county official in 1922, and then as a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">U.S. Senator</a> in 1934. He gained national prominence as chairman of the <a href="/wiki/Truman_Committee" title="Truman Committee">Truman Committee</a>, formed in March 1941, which aimed to find and correct problems such as waste and inefficiency in Federal Government wartime contracts.</p>
<p>After serving as a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senator" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Senator">United States Senator</a> from Missouri (1935–45) and briefly as <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">Vice President</a> (1945), he succeeded to the presidency on April 12, 1945, upon the death of <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>. <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Germany</a> surrendered on Truman's 61st birthday, just a few weeks after he assumed the presidency, but the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_War" title="Pacific War">war with Imperial Japan</a> raged on and was expected to last at least another year. Truman approved the use of <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic bomb">atomic bombs</a> to end the fighting and to spare the hundreds of thousands of American and Japanese lives that would inevitably be lost in the planned <a href="/wiki/Operation_Downfall" title="Operation Downfall">invasion of Japan and Japanese-held islands in the Pacific</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[4]</a></sup> Although this decision and the numerous issues that arose as a result of it remain the <a href="/wiki/Debate_over_the_atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Debate over the atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">subject of debate to this day</a>, it was one of the principal factors that forced <a href="/wiki/Surrender_of_Japan" title="Surrender of Japan">Japan's unconditional surrender</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Truman presided over an unexpected surge in economic prosperity as America sought readjustment after long years of depression and war. His presidency was a turning point in foreign affairs, as the United States engaged in an <a href="/wiki/Internationalism_(politics)" title="Internationalism (politics)">internationalist</a> foreign policy and renounced <a href="/wiki/Isolationism" title="Isolationism">isolationism</a>. Truman helped found the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> in 1945, issued the <a href="/wiki/Truman_Doctrine" title="Truman Doctrine">Truman Doctrine</a> in 1947 to contain <a href="/wiki/Communism_of_the_Soviet_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Communism of the Soviet Union">Communism</a>, and got the $13&#160;billion <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a> enacted to rebuild Western Europe. His <a href="/wiki/New_Deal_coalition" title="New Deal coalition">political coalition</a> was based on the white South, labor unions, farmers, ethnic groups, and traditional Democrats across the North. Truman was able to rally these groups of supporters during the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1948" title="United States presidential election, 1948">1948 presidential election</a> and win a surprise victory that secured a presidential term in his own right.</p>
<p>The Soviet Union became an enemy in the Cold War. Truman oversaw the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Airlift" class="mw-redirect" title="Berlin Airlift">Berlin Airlift</a> of 1948 and the creation of <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> in 1949, but was unable to stop Communists from taking over China. When communist <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a> invaded <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> in 1950, he sent U.S. troops and gained UN approval for the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[6]</a></sup> After initial successes in Korea, however, the UN forces were thrown back by Chinese intervention, and the conflict was stalemated throughout the final years of Truman's presidency. On domestic issues, bills endorsed by Truman often faced opposition from a <a href="/wiki/Conservative_coalition" title="Conservative coalition">conservative Congress</a> dominated by the Southern legislators, but his administration was able to successfully guide the American economy through the post-war economic challenges. Truman maintained that civil rights were a moral priority, and in 1948 submitted the first comprehensive civil rights legislation and issued <a href="/wiki/Executive_Order_9981" title="Executive Order 9981">Executive Orders</a> to start racial integration in the military and federal agencies. Allegations were raised of corruption in the Truman administration, linked to certain <a href="/wiki/United_States_Cabinet" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Cabinet">cabinet</a> members and senior White House staff; this became a central campaign issue in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1952" title="United States presidential election, 1952">1952 presidential election</a>, and helped account for Republican <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a>'s electoral victory. Scholars, starting in 1962, <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">ranked Truman's presidency</a> as "near great"; since then he has ranked from 5th to 9th place from the top.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_career">Early life and career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harry_S._Truman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Harry S. Truman was born on May 8, 1884, in <a href="/wiki/Lamar,_Missouri" title="Lamar, Missouri">Lamar, Missouri</a>, the oldest child of John Anderson Truman (1851–1914) and <a href="/wiki/Martha_Ellen_Young_Truman" title="Martha Ellen Young Truman">Martha Ellen Young Truman</a> (1852–1947). His parents chose the name Harry after his mother's brother, Harrison "Harry" Young (1846–1916). While the "S" did not stand for any one name, it was chosen as his middle initial to honor both of his grandfathers, Anderson Shipp Truman and Solomon Young.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough199237_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough199237-9">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETruman_Library2012_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETruman_Library2012-10">[8]</a></sup> The initial has been regularly written and printed followed by a period.<sup id="cite_ref-initialS_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-initialS-4">[b]</a></sup> A brother, John Vivian (1886–1965), was born soon after Harry, followed by sister Mary Jane (1889–1978).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough199227.2C_37_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough199227.2C_37-11">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>John Truman was a farmer and livestock dealer. The family lived in Lamar until Harry was ten months old, when they moved to a farm near <a href="/wiki/Harrisonville,_Missouri" title="Harrisonville, Missouri">Harrisonville, Missouri</a>. The family next moved to <a href="/wiki/Belton,_Missouri" title="Belton, Missouri">Belton</a>, and in 1887 to his grandparents' 600-acre (240-ha) farm in <a href="/wiki/Grandview,_Missouri" title="Grandview, Missouri">Grandview</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETruman_Library.2C_Birth2012_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETruman_Library.2C_Birth2012-12">[10]</a></sup> When Truman was six, his parents moved to <a href="/wiki/Independence,_Missouri" title="Independence, Missouri">Independence</a>, so he could attend the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian">Presbyterian</a> Church Sunday School. Truman did not attend a traditional school until he was eight.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough199237.2C_77.2C_1112_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough199237.2C_77.2C_1112-13">[11]</a></sup> While living in Independence, he served as the <a href="/wiki/Shabbos_goy" title="Shabbos goy">Shabbos goy</a> for his Jewish neighbors, doing tasks for them on Shabbos which they were prevented from doing because of rules against work.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a boy, Truman was interested in music, reading, war, and history, all encouraged by his mother, with whom he was very close. As president, he solicited political as well as personal advice from her.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOshinsky2004365.E2.80.93380_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOshinsky2004365.E2.80.93380-17">[15]</a></sup> He got up at five every morning to practice the piano, which he studied twice a week until he was fifteen.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough199238_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough199238-18">[16]</a></sup> Truman worked as a page at the <a href="/wiki/1900_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1900 Democratic National Convention">1900 Democratic National Convention</a> at <a href="/wiki/Convention_Hall" title="Convention Hall">Convention Hall</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City,_Missouri" title="Kansas City, Missouri">Kansas City</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell199487_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell199487-19">[17]</a></sup> his father had many friends who were active in the Democratic Party and helped young Harry to gain his first political position.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETruman_Library2012aa_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETruman_Library2012aa-20">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>After graduating from Independence High School (now <a href="/wiki/William_Chrisman_High_School" title="William Chrisman High School">William Chrisman High School</a>) in 1901, Truman enrolled in Spalding's Commercial College, a Kansas City business school; he studied bookkeeping, shorthand, and typing, but left after a year.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup> He made use of his business college experience to obtain a job as a timekeeper on the <a href="/wiki/Atchison,_Topeka_and_Santa_Fe_Railway" title="Atchison, Topeka and Santa Fe Railway">Atchison, Topeka &amp; Santa Fe Railway</a>, sleeping in <a href="/wiki/Hobo" title="Hobo">hobo</a> camps near the rail lines.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETruman_Library.2C_Job2012_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETruman_Library.2C_Job2012-22">[20]</a></sup> He then took on a series of clerical jobs, and was employed briefly in the mail room of the <i><a href="/wiki/Kansas_City_Star" class="mw-redirect" title="Kansas City Star">Kansas City Star</a>.</i> Truman and his brother Vivian later worked as clerks at the National Bank of Commerce in Kansas City; one of their coworkers, who also lived at the same rooming house, was Arthur Eisenhower, the brother of <a href="/wiki/Dwight_Eisenhower" class="mw-redirect" title="Dwight Eisenhower">Dwight</a> and <a href="/wiki/Milton_Eisenhower" class="mw-redirect" title="Milton Eisenhower">Milton</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup> He returned to the Grandview farm in 1906, where he lived until entering the army in 1917 after the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">Great War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough199267.2C_99_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough199267.2C_99-24">[22]</a></sup> During this period, he courted <a href="/wiki/Bess_Truman" title="Bess Truman">Bess Wallace</a>; he proposed in 1911, but she turned him down. Truman later said that he intended to propose again, but when he did he wanted to be earning more money than a farmer did.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough199278.E2.80.9379_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough199278.E2.80.9379-25">[23]</a></sup> To that end, during his years on the farm and immediately after World War I, he became active in several business ventures, including a lead and zinc mine near <a href="/wiki/Commerce,_Oklahoma" title="Commerce, Oklahoma">Commerce, Oklahoma</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup> a company that bought land and leased the oil drilling rights to prospectors,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup> and speculating in Kansas City real estate.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup> Truman occasionally derived some income from these enterprises, but none proved successful in the long term.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Truman is the most recent president who did not earn a college degree.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup> In addition to having briefly attended business college, from 1923 to 1925 he took night courses toward an <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Bachelor of laws">LL.B.</a> at the Kansas City Law School (now the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Missouri%E2%80%93Kansas_City_School_of_Law" title="University of Missouri–Kansas City School of Law">University of Missouri–Kansas City School of Law</a>), but dropped out after losing reelection as county judge.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamby199517.E2.80.9318.2C_135_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamby199517.E2.80.9318.2C_135-31">[29]</a></sup> He was informed by attorneys in the Kansas City area that his education and experience were probably sufficient to receive a license to practice law, but didn't pursue it because he won election as presiding judge.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup> While serving as president in 1947, Truman applied for a license to practice law.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup> A friend who was an attorney began working out the arrangements, and informed Truman that his application needed to be notarized. By the time Truman received this information he had changed his mind, so he never followed up. After rediscovery of Truman's application, in 1996 the <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Supreme_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="Missouri Supreme Court">Missouri Supreme Court</a> issued Truman a posthumous honorary law license.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_I">World War I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harry_S._Truman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: World War I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harry_S._Truman_WW_I.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Truman in military uniform with shoulder and waist belt with helmet" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Harry_S._Truman_WW_I.jpg/220px-Harry_S._Truman_WW_I.jpg" width="220" height="332" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b6/Harry_S._Truman_WW_I.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="323" data-file-height="488" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Harry_S._Truman_WW_I.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Truman in uniform ca. 1918</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Because he was unable to afford university tuition, Truman had thought of going to the costless <a href="/wiki/United_States_Military_Academy" title="United States Military Academy">United States Military Academy</a> at West Point, but he was refused an appointment because of poor eyesight.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamby199517.E2.80.9318.2C_135_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamby199517.E2.80.9318.2C_135-31">[29]</a></sup> He enlisted in the <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Army_National_Guard" class="mw-redirect" title="Missouri Army National Guard">Missouri Army National Guard</a> in 1905, serving until 1911 in a Kansas City-based artillery <a href="/wiki/Artillery_battery" title="Artillery battery">battery</a> and attaining the rank of <a href="/wiki/Corporal#United_States" title="Corporal">corporal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilwee2000_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilwee2000-35">[33]</a></sup> At his induction, his eyesight had been an unacceptable <a href="/wiki/Visual_acuity" title="Visual acuity">20/50</a> in the right eye and 20/400 in the left (past the standard for legal blindness).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992105_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992105-36">[34]</a></sup> The second time he took the test, he passed by secretly memorizing the eye chart.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETruman_Library.2C_Eye2012_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETruman_Library.2C_Eye2012-37">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>When the United States entered <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, Truman rejoined the National Guard; he helped recruit new soldiers as his unit expanded, and his success led the men of his battery to elect him as their <a href="/wiki/First_lieutenant#U.S._Army.2C_U.S._Marine_Corps_and_U.S._Air_Force" title="First lieutenant">first lieutenant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup> Before deployment to France, Truman was sent for training to <a href="/wiki/Camp_Doniphan,_Oklahoma" title="Camp Doniphan, Oklahoma">Camp Doniphan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fort_Sill" title="Fort Sill">Fort Sill</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Lawton,_Oklahoma" title="Lawton, Oklahoma">Lawton, Oklahoma</a>. He ran the camp <a href="/wiki/Post_exchange" class="mw-redirect" title="Post exchange">canteen</a> with <a href="/wiki/Edward_Jacobson" title="Edward Jacobson">Edward Jacobson</a>, a clothing store clerk he knew from Kansas City. Unlike most canteens funded by unit members, which usually lost money, the canteen operated by Truman and Jacobson turned a profit, returning each soldier's initial $2 investment and $10,000 in dividends in six months.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilwee2000_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilwee2000-35">[33]</a></sup> At Fort Sill, Truman met Lieutenant James M. Pendergast, nephew of <a href="/wiki/Tom_Pendergast" title="Tom Pendergast">Thomas Joseph (Tom) Pendergast</a>, a Kansas City political boss, and this connection had a profound influence on Truman's later life.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992105.E2.80.9310_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992105.E2.80.9310-39">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiangreco2012_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiangreco2012-40">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>In mid-1918, about one million soldiers of the <a href="/wiki/American_Expeditionary_Forces" title="American Expeditionary Forces">American Expeditionary Forces</a> were in France.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECurrentFreidelWilliams1971594_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECurrentFreidelWilliams1971594-41">[39]</a></sup> Truman was promoted to <a href="/wiki/Captain_(United_States_O-3)" title="Captain (United States O-3)">captain</a> in July 1918 and became commander of Battery D, <a href="/wiki/129th_Field_Artillery_Regiment_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="129th Field Artillery Regiment (United States)">129th Field Artillery</a>, 60th Artillery Brigade, <a href="/wiki/35th_Infantry_Division_(United_States)" title="35th Infantry Division (United States)">35th Division</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992115_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992115-42">[40]</a></sup> It was known for its discipline problems, and Truman was initially unpopular because of his efforts to restore order.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilwee2000_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilwee2000-35">[33]</a></sup> Despite attempts by the men to intimidate him into quitting, Truman succeeded by making his corporals and sergeants accountable for discipline; he promised to back them up if they performed capably, and reduce them to private and return them to the ranks if they did not.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurnes200349_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurnes200349-43">[41]</a></sup> In an event memorialized in battery lore as the "Battle of Who Run", his soldiers began to flee during a sudden attack by the Germans in the <a href="/wiki/Vosges_Mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Vosges Mountains">Vosges Mountains</a>; Truman succeeded at ordering his men to stay and fight, using profanity that he had first heard while working on the Santa Fe Railroad. The men were so surprised to hear Truman use such language that they immediately obeyed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilwee2000_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilwee2000-35">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Truman's unit joined in a massive pre-arranged assault barrage on September 26, 1918, at the opening of the <a href="/wiki/Meuse-Argonne_Offensive" title="Meuse-Argonne Offensive">Meuse-Argonne Offensive</a>. They advanced with difficulty over <a href="/wiki/Zone_rouge" class="mw-redirect" title="Zone rouge">pitted terrain</a> to follow the infantry, and they set up an observation post west of <a href="/wiki/Cheppy" title="Cheppy">Cheppy</a>. On September 27, Truman saw through his binoculars an enemy artillery battery setting up across a river in a position allowing them to fire upon the neighboring <a href="/wiki/28th_Division_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="28th Division (United States)">28th Division</a>. Truman's orders limited him to targets facing the 35th Division, but he ignored this and patiently waited until the Germans had walked their horses well away from their guns, ensuring that they could not relocate out of range of Truman's battery. He then ordered his men to open fire, and destroyed the enemy battery. His actions were credited with saving the lives of 28th Division soldiers who otherwise would have come under fire from the Germans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992130.2C_531_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992130.2C_531-44">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiangreco2002192_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiangreco2002192-45">[43]</a></sup> Truman was given a dressing down by his regimental commander, Colonel Karl D. Klemm, but he was not court-martialed or otherwise punished.</p>
<p>In other action during the <a href="/wiki/Meuse-Argonne_Offensive" title="Meuse-Argonne Offensive">Meuse-Argonne fighting</a>, Truman's battery provided support for <a href="/wiki/George_S._Patton" title="George S. Patton">George S. Patton</a>'s tank brigade,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiangreco2002181.E2.80.9386_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiangreco2002181.E2.80.9386-46">[44]</a></sup> and his battery fired some of the last shots of the war on November 11, 1918. Battery D did not lose any men while under Truman's command in France; to show their appreciation of his leadership, his men presented him with a large <a href="/wiki/Loving_cup" title="Loving cup">loving cup</a> upon their return to the United States after the war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilwee2000_35-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilwee2000-35">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>The war was a transformative experience for Truman that brought out his leadership qualities. He had entered the service in 1917 as a family farmer who had worked in clerical jobs that did not require the ability to motivate and direct others, but during the war he gained leadership experience and a record of success that greatly enhanced and supported his post-war political career in Missouri.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilwee2000_35-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilwee2000-35">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Truman was brought up in the Presbyterian and Baptist churches; he avoided revivals and sometimes ridiculed revivalist preachers. He rarely spoke about religion, which to him primarily meant ethical behavior along traditional Protestant lines. Most of the soldiers that he commanded in the war were Catholics; developing leadership and interpersonal skills which later made him a successful politician enabled him to get along well with them, as he did with soldiers of other Christian denominations and the unit's Jewish members.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Continued_military_service">Continued military service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harry_S._Truman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Continued military service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Truman was discharged from the Army as a <a href="/wiki/Major_(United_States)" title="Major (United States)">major</a> in May 1919.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup> In 1920 he was appointed a major in the Reserve Officer Corps; he became a <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_Colonel_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lieutenant Colonel (United States)">lieutenant colonel</a> in 1925 and a colonel in 1932.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup> In the 1920s and 1930s Truman commanded 1st Battalion, 379th Field Artillery Regiment, a unit of the <a href="/wiki/102nd_Infantry_Division_(United_States)" title="102nd Infantry Division (United States)">102nd Infantry Division</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup> After promotion to colonel, Truman advanced to command of the regiment.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>After his election to the U.S. Senate, Truman was transferred to the General Assignments Group, a holding unit for less active officers; he had not been consulted or notified in advance.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-52">[50]</a></sup> Truman protested his reassignment, which led to his resumption of regimental command.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-52">[50]</a></sup> He remained an active reservist until the early 1940s.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup> Truman volunteered for active military service during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, but was not accepted, partly because of age, and partly because President <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> desired Senators and Congressman who belonged to the military reserves to support the war effort by remaining in Congress, or by ending their active duty service and resuming their Congressional seats.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup> He was an inactive reservist from the early 1940s until retiring on January 20, 1953.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harry_S._Truman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_Jackson_County_judge">As Jackson County judge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harry_S._Truman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: As Jackson County judge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TrumanWedding.PNG" class="image"><img alt="Wedding photo of Truman in gray suit and his wife in hat with white dress holding flowers" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/TrumanWedding.PNG/170px-TrumanWedding.PNG" width="170" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/TrumanWedding.PNG/255px-TrumanWedding.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/94/TrumanWedding.PNG 2x" data-file-width="328" data-file-height="465" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TrumanWedding.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Harry and Bess Truman on their wedding day, June 28, 1919</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After his wartime service, Truman returned to Independence, where he married Bess Wallace on June 28, 1919.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETruman_Library1919_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETruman_Library1919-56">[54]</a></sup> The couple had one child, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Truman" title="Margaret Truman">Mary Margaret Truman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldstein2008_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldstein2008-57">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shortly before the wedding, Truman and Jacobson opened a haberdashery together at 104 West 12th Street in downtown <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City,_Missouri" title="Kansas City, Missouri">Kansas City</a>. After brief initial success, the store went bankrupt during the <a href="/wiki/Post-World_War_I_recession" class="mw-redirect" title="Post-World War I recession">recession of 1921</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOshinsky2004365.E2.80.93380_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOshinsky2004365.E2.80.93380-17">[15]</a></sup> Truman did not pay off the last of the debts from that venture until 1934, when he did so with the aid of a political supporter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough199263.E2.80.9364.2C_68_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough199263.E2.80.9364.2C_68-58">[56]</a></sup> Jacobson and Truman remained close friends, and Jacobson's advice to Truman on <a href="/wiki/Zionism" title="Zionism">Zionism</a> later played a role in the U.S. government's decision to recognize Israel.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamby1995410.E2.80.9312_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamby1995410.E2.80.9312-59">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the help of the Kansas City <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic</a> <a href="/wiki/Political_machine" title="Political machine">machine</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Tom_Pendergast" title="Tom Pendergast">Tom Pendergast</a>, Truman was elected in 1922 as County Court judge of <a href="/wiki/Jackson_County,_Missouri" title="Jackson County, Missouri">Jackson County</a>'s eastern district—this was an administrative rather than judicial position, somewhat similar to county commissioners elsewhere. (At the time Jackson County elected a judge from the western district (Kansas City), one from the eastern district (Jackson County outside Kansas City), and a presiding judge elected countywide.)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOshinsky2004365.E2.80.9380_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOshinsky2004365.E2.80.9380-60">[58]</a></sup> Truman was not re-elected in 1924, losing in a Republican wave led by President <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge">Calvin Coolidge</a>'s <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1924" title="United States presidential election, 1924">landslide election</a> to a full term. Two years selling automobile club memberships convinced him that a public service career was safer for a family man approaching middle age, and he planned a run for presiding judge in 1926.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek20086_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek20086-61">[59]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Jackson_statue_County_Courthouse_KC_Missouri.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Andrew_Jackson_statue_County_Courthouse_KC_Missouri.jpg/300px-Andrew_Jackson_statue_County_Courthouse_KC_Missouri.jpg" width="300" height="394" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Andrew_Jackson_statue_County_Courthouse_KC_Missouri.jpg/450px-Andrew_Jackson_statue_County_Courthouse_KC_Missouri.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Andrew_Jackson_statue_County_Courthouse_KC_Missouri.jpg/600px-Andrew_Jackson_statue_County_Courthouse_KC_Missouri.jpg 2x" data-file-width="653" data-file-height="857" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Jackson_statue_County_Courthouse_KC_Missouri.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Statue by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Keck" title="Charles Keck">Charles Keck</a> of <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> outside the Jackson County Courthouse that was installed by Truman.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1926, Truman was elected <a href="/wiki/List_of_county_executives_of_Jackson_County,_Missouri" title="List of county executives of Jackson County, Missouri">presiding judge</a> with the support of the Pendergast machine, and he was re-elected in 1930. Truman helped coordinate the "Ten Year Plan", which transformed Jackson County and the Kansas City skyline with new public works projects, including an extensive series of roads and construction of a new <a href="/wiki/Wight_and_Wight" title="Wight and Wight">Wight and Wight</a>-designed County Court building. Also in 1926, he became president of the <a href="/wiki/National_Old_Trails_Road" title="National Old Trails Road">National Old Trails Road</a> Association (NOTRA). He oversaw the dedication in the late 1920s of a series of 12 <a href="/wiki/Madonna_of_the_Trail" title="Madonna of the Trail">Madonna of the Trail</a> monuments honoring pioneer women, which were installed along the trail.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek20086_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek20086-61">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarr2004_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarr2004-62">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1933, Truman was named Missouri's director for the Federal Re-Employment program (part of the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Works_Administration" title="Civil Works Administration">Civil Works Administration</a>) at the request of Postmaster General <a href="/wiki/James_Farley" title="James Farley">James Farley</a>. This was payback to Pendergast for delivering the Kansas City vote to <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1932" title="United States presidential election, 1932">1932 presidential election</a>. The appointment confirmed Pendergast's control over federal <a href="/wiki/Patronage" title="Patronage">patronage</a> jobs in Missouri and marked the zenith of his power. It also created a relationship between Truman and Roosevelt aide <a href="/wiki/Harry_Hopkins" title="Harry Hopkins">Harry Hopkins</a> and assured Truman's avid support for the New Deal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESavage199165_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESavage199165-63">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_U.S._Senator_from_Missouri">As U.S. Senator from Missouri</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harry_S._Truman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: As U.S. Senator from Missouri">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Senate_Desk_Truman.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Inside of wooden desk with several names carved into it" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Senate_Desk_Truman.jpg/220px-Senate_Desk_Truman.jpg" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Senate_Desk_Truman.jpg/330px-Senate_Desk_Truman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Senate_Desk_Truman.jpg/440px-Senate_Desk_Truman.jpg 2x" data-file-width="537" data-file-height="428" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Senate_Desk_Truman.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Drawer from the Senate desk used by Truman</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After serving as a county judge, Truman wanted to run for Governor or Congress, but Pendergast rejected these ideas. Truman then thought that he might serve out his career in some well-paying county sinecure, but circumstances changed when Pendergast reluctantly backed him in the 1934 Democratic <a href="/wiki/Primary_election" title="Primary election">primary</a> for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_election_in_Missouri,_1934" title="United States Senate election in Missouri, 1934">U.S. Senate</a> after four other potential candidates turned him down.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnited_States_Senate2012_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnited_States_Senate2012-64">[62]</a></sup> In the primary, Truman defeated Congressmen <a href="/wiki/John_J._Cochran" title="John J. Cochran">John J. Cochran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jacob_L._Milligan" title="Jacob L. Milligan">Jacob L. Milligan</a> with the solid support of <a href="/wiki/Jackson_County,_Missouri" title="Jackson County, Missouri">Jackson County</a>, which was crucial to his candidacy, as were the contacts that he had made statewide as a county official, Mason, military reservist, and member of the American Legion.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup> In the general election, Truman defeated incumbent Republican <a href="/wiki/Roscoe_C._Patterson" title="Roscoe C. Patterson">Roscoe C. Patterson</a> by nearly 20 percentage points as part of a continuing wave of pro-<a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a> Democrats elected in response to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnited_States_Senate2012_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnited_States_Senate2012-64">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek20087.E2.80.939_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek20087.E2.80.939-66">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinn2000_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinn2000-67">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Truman assumed office with a reputation as "the Senator from Pendergast." He turned over patronage decisions to Pendergast, though Truman always maintained that he voted his conscience. He later defended the patronage decisions by saying that "by offering a little to the machine, [he] saved a lot".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinn2000_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinn2000-67">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27Time.27.27January_8.2C_1973_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27Time.27.27January_8.2C_1973-68">[66]</a></sup> In his first term, Truman spoke out against corporate greed and the dangers of <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street" title="Wall Street">Wall Street</a> speculators and other moneyed special interests attaining too much influence in national affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992232_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992232-69">[67]</a></sup> He was largely ignored by Democratic President Roosevelt and had trouble getting calls returned from the White House.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinn2000_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinn2000-67">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992230_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992230-70">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_election_in_Missouri,_1940" title="United States Senate election in Missouri, 1940">U.S. Senate election in 1940</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Attorney" title="United States Attorney">United States Attorney</a> Maurice Milligan (Jacob Milligan's brother) and former governor <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Stark" class="mw-redirect" title="Lloyd Stark">Lloyd Stark</a> both challenged Truman in the Democratic primary. Truman was politically weakened by Pendergast's imprisonment for income tax evasion the previous year; the senator had remained loyal, having claimed that Republican judges (not the Roosevelt administration) were responsible for the boss's downfall.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek200811.E2.80.9312_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek200811.E2.80.9312-71">[69]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Louis, Missouri">St. Louis</a> party leader <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Hannegan" title="Robert E. Hannegan">Robert E. Hannegan</a>'s support of Truman proved crucial; he later brokered the deal that put Truman on the national ticket. In the end, Stark and Milligan split the anti-Pendergast vote in the Senate Democratic primary and Truman won by a total of 8,000 votes. In the November election, Truman defeated Republican <a href="/wiki/Manvel_H._Davis" title="Manvel H. Davis">Manvel H. Davis</a> by 51–49 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamby1995236.E2.80.9347_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamby1995236.E2.80.9347-72">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>In late 1940, Truman traveled to various military bases. The waste and profiteering which he saw led him to use his subcommittee chairmanship in the Committee on Military Affairs to begin investigations into abuses while the nation prepared for war. A separate committee was set up under Truman to conduct a formal investigation; the <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt_Administration" class="mw-redirect" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt Administration">Roosevelt administration</a> supported this plan rather than weather a more hostile probe by the House of Representatives. Chairmanship of what came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Truman_Committee" title="Truman Committee">Truman Committee</a> made him a national figure.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek200812.E2.80.9314_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek200812.E2.80.9314-73">[71]</a></sup> Activities of the Truman Committee ranged from criticizing the "<a href="/wiki/Dollar-a-year_men" class="mw-redirect" title="Dollar-a-year men">dollar-a-year men</a>" hired by the government, many of whom proved ineffective, to investigating a shoddily built New Jersey housing project for war workers.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27Life.27.27November_30.2C_1942_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27Life.27.27November_30.2C_1942-75">[73]</a></sup> The committee is reported to have saved as much as $15&#160;billion;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992337.E2.80.9338_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992337.E2.80.9338-76">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcDonald1984_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcDonald1984-77">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaniels1998228_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaniels1998228-78">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton2009301_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton2009301-79">[77]</a></sup> its activities put Truman on the cover of <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27Time.27.272012_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27Time.27.272012-80">[78]</a></sup> According to the Senate's historical minutes, in leading the committee, "Truman erased his earlier public image as an errand-runner for Kansas City politicos" and "no senator ever gained greater political benefits from chairing a special investigating committee than did Missouri's Harry S. Truman."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESenate_Truman_Committee2012_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESenate_Truman_Committee2012-81">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>Senator Truman opposed both Nazi Germany as well as Communist Russia. One week after <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">Hitler invaded the Soviet Union</a> in 1941, he said:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>If we see that Germany is winning we ought to help Russia, and if Russia is winning we ought to help Germany, and that way let them kill as many as possible although I don't want to see Hitler victorious under any circumstances.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vice_presidency_.281945.29">Vice presidency (1945)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harry_S._Truman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Vice presidency (1945)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Truman-Mother-LIFE-1944.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Truman-Mother-LIFE-1944.jpg/220px-Truman-Mother-LIFE-1944.jpg" width="220" height="237" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Truman-Mother-LIFE-1944.jpg/330px-Truman-Mother-LIFE-1944.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Truman-Mother-LIFE-1944.jpg/440px-Truman-Mother-LIFE-1944.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1186" data-file-height="1277" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Sen. Truman visits <a href="/wiki/Martha_Ellen_Young_Truman" title="Martha Ellen Young Truman">his mother</a> in <a href="/wiki/Grandview,_Missouri" title="Grandview, Missouri">Grandview, Missouri</a>, after being <a href="/wiki/Democratic_vice_presidential_nomination_of_1944" class="mw-redirect" title="Democratic vice presidential nomination of 1944">nominated the Democratic candidate for vice president</a> (July 1944)</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:RooseveltTruman1944poster.jpg" class="image"><img alt="election poster from 1944 with Roosevelt and Truman" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/RooseveltTruman1944poster.jpg/220px-RooseveltTruman1944poster.jpg" width="220" height="281" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/RooseveltTruman1944poster.jpg/330px-RooseveltTruman1944poster.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a5/RooseveltTruman1944poster.jpg 2x" data-file-width="348" data-file-height="445" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Roosevelt</a>/Truman poster from <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1944" title="United States presidential election, 1944">1944</a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Bacall-Truman-Piano-1945.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Bacall-Truman-Piano-1945.jpg/220px-Bacall-Truman-Piano-1945.jpg" width="220" height="283" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Bacall-Truman-Piano-1945.jpg/330px-Bacall-Truman-Piano-1945.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Bacall-Truman-Piano-1945.jpg/440px-Bacall-Truman-Piano-1945.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1228" data-file-height="1578" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Lauren_Bacall" title="Lauren Bacall">Lauren Bacall</a> lounges on top of the piano while Vice President Truman plays for servicemen at the <a href="/wiki/National_Press_Club_(United_States)" title="National Press Club (United States)">National Press Club</a> Canteen in Washington, D.C. (February 10, 1945)</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Democratic_vice_presidential_nomination_of_1944" class="mw-redirect" title="Democratic vice presidential nomination of 1944">Democratic vice presidential nomination of 1944</a></div>
<p>Vice President <a href="/wiki/Henry_A._Wallace" title="Henry A. Wallace">Henry Wallace</a> was popular among Democratic voters, but he was viewed as too far to the left and too friendly to labor for some of Roosevelt's advisers. The President and several of his confidantes wanted to replace Wallace with someone more acceptable to Democratic Party leaders and Roosevelt's advisors, knowing that Roosevelt might not live out a fourth term. Outgoing Democratic National Committee chairman <a href="/wiki/Frank_C._Walker" class="mw-redirect" title="Frank C. Walker">Frank C. Walker</a>, incoming chairman Hannegan, party treasurer <a href="/wiki/Edwin_W._Pauley" title="Edwin W. Pauley">Edwin W. Pauley</a>, strategist <a href="/wiki/Edward_J._Flynn" title="Edward J. Flynn">Ed Flynn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_Chicago" title="Mayor of Chicago">Chicago Mayor</a> <a href="/wiki/Edward_Joseph_Kelly" title="Edward Joseph Kelly">Edward Joseph Kelly</a>, and lobbyist George E. Allen all wanted to keep Wallace off the ticket.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992373.E2.80.93378_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992373.E2.80.93378-83">[81]</a></sup> Roosevelt told party leaders that he would accept either Truman or <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court</a> Justice <a href="/wiki/William_O._Douglas" title="William O. Douglas">William O. Douglas</a>. State and city party leaders strongly preferred Truman, and Roosevelt agreed. Truman did not campaign for the Vice-Presidential spot, though he welcomed the attention as evidence that he had become more than the "Senator from Pendergast".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek200814.E2.80.9316_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek200814.E2.80.9316-84">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>Truman's nomination was dubbed the "Second <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Compromise" title="Missouri Compromise">Missouri Compromise</a>" and was well received. The Roosevelt–Truman ticket achieved a 432–99 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Electoral_College" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Electoral College">electoral-vote</a> victory in the election, defeating the Republican ticket of Governor <a href="/wiki/Thomas_E._Dewey" title="Thomas E. Dewey">Thomas E. Dewey</a> of New York and running mate Governor <a href="/wiki/John_Bricker" class="mw-redirect" title="John Bricker">John Bricker</a> of Ohio. Truman was sworn in as vice president on January 20, 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek200815.E2.80.9317_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek200815.E2.80.9317-85">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>Truman's brief vice-presidency was relatively uneventful. On April 10, 1945,<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup> Truman cast his only <a href="/wiki/List_of_tie-breaking_votes_cast_by_Vice_Presidents_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="List of tie-breaking votes cast by Vice Presidents of the United States">tie-breaking vote</a> as President of the Senate, against a <a href="/wiki/Robert_A._Taft" title="Robert A. Taft">Robert A. Taft</a> amendment that would have blocked the postwar delivery of <a href="/wiki/Lend-Lease_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Lend-Lease Act">Lend-Lease Act</a> items contracted for during the war.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup> Roosevelt rarely contacted him, even to inform him of major decisions; the President and Vice President met alone together only twice during their time in office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek200816_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek200816-89">[87]</a></sup> In one of his first acts as vice president, Truman created some controversy when he attended the disgraced Pendergast's funeral. He brushed aside the criticism, saying simply, "He was always my friend and I have always been his."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOshinsky2004365.E2.80.93380_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOshinsky2004365.E2.80.93380-17">[15]</a></sup> He had rarely discussed world affairs or domestic politics with Roosevelt; he was uninformed about major initiatives relating to the war and the top-secret <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>, which was about to test the world's first atomic bomb.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEU.S._History2012_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEU.S._History2012-90">[88]</a></sup> He was also photographed with actress <a href="/wiki/Lauren_Bacall" title="Lauren Bacall">Lauren Bacall</a> sitting atop the piano at the <a href="/wiki/National_Press_Club_(United_States)" title="National Press Club (United States)">National Press Club</a> as he played for soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Truman had been vice president for 82 days when President Roosevelt died on April 12, 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEU.S._History2012_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEU.S._History2012-90">[88]</a></sup> That afternoon, Truman presided over the Senate as usual. He had just adjourned the session for the day and was preparing to have a drink in <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">House Speaker</a> <a href="/wiki/Sam_Rayburn" title="Sam Rayburn">Sam Rayburn</a>'s office when he received an urgent message to go immediately to the White House. Truman assumed that President Roosevelt wanted to meet with him, but <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Roosevelt" title="Eleanor Roosevelt">Eleanor Roosevelt</a> informed him that her husband had died after suffering a massive <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_hemorrhage" class="mw-redirect" title="Cerebral hemorrhage">cerebral hemorrhage</a>. Truman's first concern was for Mrs. Roosevelt. He asked if there was anything that he could do for her, to which she replied, "Is there anything we can do for you? For you are the one in trouble now!"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETruman_Library2012h_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETruman_Library2012h-92">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992425_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992425-93">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwin1994478_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwin1994478-94">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_.281945-53.29">Presidency (1945-53)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harry_S._Truman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Presidency (1945-53)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Harry_S._Truman" title="Presidency of Harry S. Truman">Presidency of Harry S. Truman</a></div>
<p>Truman surrounded himself with his old friends, and appointed several to high positions that seemed well beyond their competence, including his two secretaries of the treasury, <a href="/wiki/Fred_Vinson" class="mw-redirect" title="Fred Vinson">Fred Vinson</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Wesley_Snyder_(US_Cabinet_Secretary)" title="John Wesley Snyder (US Cabinet Secretary)">John Snyder</a>. His closest friend in the White House was his military aide <a href="/wiki/Harry_H._Vaughan" title="Harry H. Vaughan">Harry H. Vaughan</a>, who seemed to others like a huge joke.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992366_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992366-95">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamby1995301-02.2C_472_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamby1995301-02.2C_472-96">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>Truman loved to spend as much time as possible playing poker, telling stories and sipping bourbon. Alonzo Hamby notes that:</p>
<dl>
<dd>to many in the general public, gambling and bourbon swilling, however low-key, were not quite presidential. Neither was the intemperant "give 'em hell" campaign style nor the occasional profane phrase uttered in public. Poker exemplified a larger problem: the tension between his attempts at an image of leadership necessarily a cut above the ordinary and an informality that at times appeared to verge on crudeness.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamby1995474_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamby1995474-97">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992511_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992511-98">[96]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_term_.281945.E2.80.9349.29">First term (1945–49)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harry_S._Truman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: First term (1945–49)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Assuming_office_and_the_atomic_bomb">Assuming office and the atomic bomb</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harry_S._Truman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Assuming office and the atomic bomb">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-29645-0001,_Potsdamer_Konferenz,_Stalin,_Truman,_Churchill.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Three men in suits standing with several men in the background" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-29645-0001%2C_Potsdamer_Konferenz%2C_Stalin%2C_Truman%2C_Churchill.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-29645-0001%2C_Potsdamer_Konferenz%2C_Stalin%2C_Truman%2C_Churchill.jpg" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-29645-0001%2C_Potsdamer_Konferenz%2C_Stalin%2C_Truman%2C_Churchill.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-29645-0001%2C_Potsdamer_Konferenz%2C_Stalin%2C_Truman%2C_Churchill.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-29645-0001%2C_Potsdamer_Konferenz%2C_Stalin%2C_Truman%2C_Churchill.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-29645-0001%2C_Potsdamer_Konferenz%2C_Stalin%2C_Truman%2C_Churchill.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2922" data-file-height="2335" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-29645-0001,_Potsdamer_Konferenz,_Stalin,_Truman,_Churchill.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a>, Harry S. Truman, and <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> in Potsdam, July 1945</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shortly after taking the oath of office, Truman spoke to reporters: "Boys, if you ever pray, pray for me now. I don't know if you fellas ever had a load of hay fall on you, but when they told me what happened yesterday, I felt like the moon, the stars, and all the planets had fallen on me."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992436_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992436-99">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Upon assuming the presidency, Truman asked all the members of Roosevelt's cabinet to remain in place, and told them that he was open to their advice. He emphasized a central principle of his administration: he would be the one making decisions, and they were to support him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992348_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992348-100">[98]</a></sup> Although Truman was told briefly on the afternoon of April 12 that the Allies had a new, highly destructive weapon, it was not until April 25 that <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_War" title="United States Secretary of War">Secretary of War</a> <a href="/wiki/Henry_Stimson" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry Stimson">Henry Stimson</a> told him the details. Truman benefited from a honeymoon period after Roosevelt's death, and from the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allies'</a> success in Europe, wrapping up the war there. Truman was pleased to issue the proclamation of <a href="/wiki/Victory_in_Europe_Day" title="Victory in Europe Day">V-E Day</a> on May 8, 1945, his 61st birthday.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy198421.E2.80.9322_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy198421.E2.80.9322-101">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek200819.E2.80.9320_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek200819.E2.80.9320-102">[100]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>We have discovered the most terrible bomb in the history of the world. It may be the fire destruction prophesied in the Euphrates Valley Era, after Noah and his fabulous Ark.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Harry Truman, writing about the atomic bomb in his diary<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReynolds2005_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReynolds2005-103">[101]</a></sup> on July 25, 1945<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>In the wake of Allied victory, Truman journeyed to Europe for the <a href="/wiki/Potsdam_Conference" title="Potsdam Conference">Potsdam Conference</a>. He was there when he learned that the <a href="/wiki/Trinity_(nuclear_test)" title="Trinity (nuclear test)">Trinity test</a> of the first atomic bomb on July 16 had been successful. He hinted to <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a> that the U.S. was about to use a new kind of weapon against the Japanese. Though this was the first time the Soviets had been officially given information about the atomic bomb, Stalin was already aware of the bomb project, having learned about it (through <a href="/wiki/Atomic_spies" title="Atomic spies">espionage</a>) long before Truman did.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPBS2012_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPBS2012-105">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETruman1955416_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETruman1955416-106">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy198437_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy198437-107">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>In August, the Japanese government refused surrender demands as specifically outlined in the <a href="/wiki/Potsdam_Declaration" title="Potsdam Declaration">Potsdam Declaration</a> and with the <a href="/wiki/Operation_Downfall" title="Operation Downfall">invasion of mainland Japan</a> imminent, Truman approved the schedule for dropping the two available bombs. Truman always said that attacking Japan with atomic bombs saved many lives on both sides; military estimates for the invasion of mainland Japan were that it could take a year and result in 250,000 to 500,000 American casualties. Hiroshima was bombed on August 6, and Nagasaki three days later, leaving 105,000 dead.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup> The Soviet Union <a href="/wiki/Soviet-Japanese_War_(1945)" class="mw-redirect" title="Soviet-Japanese War (1945)">declared war on Japan</a> on August 9 and <a href="/wiki/Soviet_invasion_of_Manchuria" title="Soviet invasion of Manchuria">invaded Manchuria</a>. Japan <a href="/wiki/Surrender_of_Japan" title="Surrender of Japan">agreed to surrender</a> the following day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller1974227.E2.80.9331_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller1974227.E2.80.9331-109">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek200824.E2.80.9328_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek200824.E2.80.9328-110">[108]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Photograph_of_President_Truman_in_the_Oval_Office,_reading_the_announcement_of_Japan%27s_surrender_to_assembled..._-_NARA_-_199171.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Photograph_of_President_Truman_in_the_Oval_Office%2C_reading_the_announcement_of_Japan%27s_surrender_to_assembled..._-_NARA_-_199171.jpg/220px-Photograph_of_President_Truman_in_the_Oval_Office%2C_reading_the_announcement_of_Japan%27s_surrender_to_assembled..._-_NARA_-_199171.jpg" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Photograph_of_President_Truman_in_the_Oval_Office%2C_reading_the_announcement_of_Japan%27s_surrender_to_assembled..._-_NARA_-_199171.jpg/330px-Photograph_of_President_Truman_in_the_Oval_Office%2C_reading_the_announcement_of_Japan%27s_surrender_to_assembled..._-_NARA_-_199171.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Photograph_of_President_Truman_in_the_Oval_Office%2C_reading_the_announcement_of_Japan%27s_surrender_to_assembled..._-_NARA_-_199171.jpg/440px-Photograph_of_President_Truman_in_the_Oval_Office%2C_reading_the_announcement_of_Japan%27s_surrender_to_assembled..._-_NARA_-_199171.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2409" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Photograph_of_President_Truman_in_the_Oval_Office,_reading_the_announcement_of_Japan%27s_surrender_to_assembled..._-_NARA_-_199171.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Truman announces Japan's surrender. Washington, DC, August 14, 1945</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Supporters<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[c]</a></sup> of Truman's decision argue that, given the tenacious Japanese defense of the outlying islands, the bombings saved hundreds of thousands of lives that would have been lost invading mainland Japan. Critics have argued that the use of nuclear weapons was unnecessary, given that conventional tactics such as firebombing and a naval blockade might have induced Japan's surrender without the need for such weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[109]</a></sup> Truman strongly defended himself in his memoirs in 1955–56, stating that many lives could have been lost had the U.S. invaded mainland Japan without the atomic bombs. In 1963, he stood by his decision, telling a journalist that "it was done to save 125,000 youngsters on the American side and 125,000 on the Japanese side from getting killed and that is what it did. It probably also saved a half million youngsters on both sides from being maimed for life."<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[110]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Strikes_and_economic_upheaval">Strikes and economic upheaval</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harry_S._Truman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Strikes and economic upheaval">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Strike_wave_of_1946" class="mw-redirect" title="Strike wave of 1946">Strike wave of 1946</a></div>
<p>The end of World War II was followed by an uneasy transition from war to a peacetime economy. The costs of the war effort were enormous, and Truman was intent on decreasing government expenditures on the military as quickly as possible. Demobilizing the military and reducing the size of the various services was a cost-saving priority. The effect of demobilization on the economy was unknown, but fears existed that the nation would slide back into a depression. A great deal of work had to be done to plan how best to transition to peacetime production of goods while avoiding mass unemployment for returning veterans. Government officials did not have consensus as to what economic course the postwar U.S. should take. In addition, Roosevelt had not paid attention to Congress in his final years, and Truman faced a body where a combination of Republicans and conservative southern Democrats formed a powerful voting bloc.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy198442.E2.80.9344_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy198442.E2.80.9344-114">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>The president was faced with the reawakening of labor-management conflicts that had lain dormant during the war years, severe shortages in housing and consumer products, and widespread dissatisfaction with inflation, which at one point hit 6% in a single month.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller_Center2012_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller_Center2012-115">[112]</a></sup> Added to this polarized environment was a wave of destabilizing strikes in major industries. Truman's response to them was generally seen as ineffective.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller_Center2012_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller_Center2012-115">[112]</a></sup> A rapid increase in costs was fueled by the release of price controls on most items, and labor sought wage increases. A serious steel strike in January 1946 involving 800,000 workers—the largest in the nation's history—was followed by a coal strike in April and a rail strike in May. The public was angry, with a majority in polls favoring a ban on strikes by public service workers and a year's moratorium on labor actions. For commodities where price controls remained, producers were often unwilling to sell at artificially low prices: farmers refused to sell grain for months in 1945 and 1946 until payments were significantly increased, even though grain was desperately needed, not only for domestic use, but to stave off starvation in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy198454.E2.80.9355_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy198454.E2.80.9355-116">[113]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Truman_with_Greek_sponge_divers..jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/President_Truman_with_Greek_sponge_divers..jpg/220px-President_Truman_with_Greek_sponge_divers..jpg" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/President_Truman_with_Greek_sponge_divers..jpg/330px-President_Truman_with_Greek_sponge_divers..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d1/President_Truman_with_Greek_sponge_divers..jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="324" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Truman_with_Greek_sponge_divers..jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Truman with <a href="/wiki/Greek_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek American">Greek American</a> sponge divers, Florida, 1947</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When a national rail strike threatened in May 1946, Truman seized the railroads. Two key railway unions struck anyway and the entire national railroad system was shut down—24,000 freight trains and 175,000 passenger trains a day stopped moving.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[114]</a></sup> For two days public anger mounted and no one was angrier than Truman himself. He drafted a message to Congress that called on veterans to form a lynch mob and destroy the union leaders:</p>
<dl>
<dd>Every single one of the strikers and their demagogue leaders have been living in luxury. . . . Now I want you who are my comrades in arms . . . to come with me and eliminate the Lewises, the Whitneys, the Johnstons, the Communist Bridges [all important union officials] and the Russian Senators and Representatives . . . Let's put transportation and production back to work, hang a few traitors and make our own country safe for democracy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992498-501_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992498-501-118">[115]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>His staff was stunned; top aide <a href="/wiki/Clark_Clifford" title="Clark Clifford">Clark Clifford</a> rewrote and toned down the speech. Truman did go to Congress and he called for a new law to draft all the railroad strikers into the Army. As he was concluding his speech he read a message just handed to him that the strike was settled on presidential terms. Truman nevertheless finished the speech, and a few hours later the House voted to draft the strikers. Taft killed the bill in the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992501-6_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992501-6-119">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although labor strife was muted after the settlement of the railway strike, it continued through Truman's presidency. The President's approval rating dropped from 82% in the polls in January 1946 to 52% by June.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy198464.E2.80.9365_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy198464.E2.80.9365-121">[118]</a></sup> This dissatisfaction with the Truman administration's policies led to large Democratic losses in the 1946 midterm elections, when Republicans took control of Congress for the first time since 1930. The <a href="/wiki/80th_United_States_Congress" title="80th United States Congress">80th Congress</a> included Republican freshmen who would become prominent in the years to come, including Wisconsin Senator <a href="/wiki/Joe_McCarthy" class="mw-redirect" title="Joe McCarthy">Joe McCarthy</a> and California Congressman <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a>. When Truman dropped to 32% in the polls, Democratic Arkansas Senator <a href="/wiki/William_Fulbright" class="mw-redirect" title="William Fulbright">William Fulbright</a> suggested that Truman resign; the President said that he did not care what Senator "Halfbright" said.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek200848.E2.80.9350_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek200848.E2.80.9350-122">[119]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy198491_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy198491-123">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>Truman cooperated closely with the Republican leaders on foreign policy, though he fought them bitterly on domestic issues. The power of the labor unions was significantly curtailed by the <a href="/wiki/Taft%E2%80%93Hartley_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Taft–Hartley Act">Taft–Hartley Act</a>, which was enacted <a href="/wiki/Veto_override" class="mw-redirect" title="Veto override">over Truman's veto</a>.
