</div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">Harry S. Truman's speech on leaving office, and returning home to Independence, Missouri. (January 15, 1953)</div>
</div>
<hr /></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Eisenhower gained the Republican nomination, with Senator Nixon as his running mate, and campaigned against what he denounced as Truman's failures: "Korea, Communism and Corruption". He pledged to clean up the "mess in Washington," and promised to "go to Korea."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992887_220-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992887-220">[217]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmbrose1983515_221-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmbrose1983515-221">[218]</a></sup> Eisenhower defeated Stevenson decisively in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1952" title="United States presidential election, 1952">general election</a>, ending 20 years of Democratic presidents. While Truman and Eisenhower had previously been on good terms, Truman felt annoyed that Eisenhower did not denounce Joseph McCarthy during the campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27Time.27.27November_10.2C_2008_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27Time.27.27November_10.2C_2008-223">[220]</a></sup> Similarly, Eisenhower was outraged when Truman accused the former general of disregarding "sinister forces&#160;... Anti-Semitism, anti-Catholicism, and anti-foreignism" within the Republican Party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek2008144_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek2008144-224">[221]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-presidency">Post-presidency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harry_S._Truman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Post-presidency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HarryTruman.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Painting of Caucasian man in dark suit with wire glasses and gray hair" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/HarryTruman.jpg/170px-HarryTruman.jpg" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/HarryTruman.jpg/255px-HarryTruman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/HarryTruman.jpg/340px-HarryTruman.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2370" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HarryTruman.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Official White House portrait of Harry S. Truman by <a href="/wiki/Greta_Kempton" title="Greta Kempton">Greta Kempton</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Upon leaving the presidency, Truman returned to Independence, Missouri, to live at the <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman_National_Historic_Site" title="Harry S. Truman National Historic Site">Wallace home</a> he and Bess had shared for years with her mother.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETruman_Library2012i_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETruman_Library2012i-225">[222]</a></sup> Once out of office, Truman quickly decided that he did not wish to be on any corporate payroll, believing that taking advantage of such financial opportunities would diminish the integrity of the nation's highest office. He also turned down numerous offers for commercial endorsements. Since his earlier business ventures had proved unsuccessful, he had no personal savings. As a result, he faced financial challenges. Once Truman left the White House, his only income was his old army pension: $112.56 per month.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVaccaro1953_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVaccaro1953-226">[223]</a></sup> Former members of Congress and the federal courts received a federal retirement package; President Truman himself ensured that former servants of the executive branch of government received similar support. In 1953, however, there was no such benefit package for former presidents,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2008_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2008-227">[224]</a></sup> and he received no pension for his Senate service.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek2008150_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek2008150-228">[225]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Medicare_bill,_with_Harry_Truman,_July_30,_1965.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Two men at a desk with a document one is signing with their wives standing behind them" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Medicare_bill%2C_with_Harry_Truman%2C_July_30%2C_1965.jpg/220px-Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Medicare_bill%2C_with_Harry_Truman%2C_July_30%2C_1965.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Medicare_bill%2C_with_Harry_Truman%2C_July_30%2C_1965.jpg/330px-Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Medicare_bill%2C_with_Harry_Truman%2C_July_30%2C_1965.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Medicare_bill%2C_with_Harry_Truman%2C_July_30%2C_1965.jpg/440px-Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Medicare_bill%2C_with_Harry_Truman%2C_July_30%2C_1965.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4850" data-file-height="3242" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Medicare_bill,_with_Harry_Truman,_July_30,_1965.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Truman (seated right) and his wife Bess (behind him) attend the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Medicare_(United_States)" title="Medicare (United States)">Medicare</a> Bill on July 30, 1965, by President <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Truman took out a personal loan from a Missouri bank shortly after leaving office, and then found a lucrative book deal for his memoirs. For the memoirs, Truman received only a flat payment of $670,000, and had to pay two-thirds of that in tax; he calculated he got $37,000 after he paid his assistants.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell1994387_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell1994387-229">[226]</a></sup> However, the memoirs were a commercial and critical success;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27Time.27.27August_13.2C_1956_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27Time.27.27August_13.2C_1956-230">[227]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nevins_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevins-231">[228]</a></sup> they were published in two volumes in 1955 and 1956 by Doubleday (Garden City, N.Y) and Hodder &amp; Stoughton (London): <i>Memoirs by Harry S. Truman: Year of Decisions</i> and <i>Memoirs by Harry S. Truman: Years of Trial and Hope</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETruman1955title_page_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETruman1955title_page-232">[229]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETruman1956title_page_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETruman1956title_page-233">[230]</a></sup></p>
<p>The former president was quoted in 1957 as saying to then-House Majority Leader <a href="/wiki/John_William_McCormack" title="John William McCormack">John McCormack</a>, "Had it not been for the fact that I was able to sell some property that my brother, sister, and I inherited from our mother, I would practically be on relief, but with the sale of that property I am not financially embarrassed."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992963_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992963-234">[231]</a></sup> The following year, Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Former_Presidents_Act" title="Former Presidents Act">Former Presidents Act</a>, offering a $25,000 yearly pension to each former president, and it is likely that Truman's financial status played a role in the law's enactment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2008_227-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2008-227">[224]</a></sup> The one other living former president at the time, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a>, also took the pension, even though he did not need the money; reportedly, he did so to avoid embarrassing Truman.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartin1960249_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartin1960249-235">[232]</a></sup></p>
<p>Truman's predecessor, Franklin D. Roosevelt, had organized his own <a href="/wiki/Presidential_library" title="Presidential library">presidential library</a>, but legislation to enable future presidents to do something similar had not been enacted. Truman worked to garner private donations to build a presidential library, which he donated to the federal government to maintain and operate—a practice adopted by his successors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurnes2003217.E2.80.9318_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurnes2003217.E2.80.9318-236">[233]</a></sup> He testified before Congress to have money appropriated to have presidential papers copied and organized, and was proud of the bill's passage in 1957. Max Skidmore, in his book on the life of former presidents, noted that Truman was a well-read man, especially in history. Skidmore added that the presidential papers legislation and the founding of his library "was the culmination of his interest in history. Together they constitute an enormous contribution to the United States—one of the greatest of any former president."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESkidmore2004123.E2.80.93124_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESkidmore2004123.E2.80.93124-237">[234]</a></sup></p>
<p>Truman supported Adlai Stevenson's second bid for the White House in 1956, although he had initially favored Democratic Governor <a href="/wiki/W._Averell_Harriman" title="W. Averell Harriman">W. Averell Harriman</a> of New York.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOhio_State2012_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOhio_State2012-238">[235]</a></sup> He continued to campaign for Democratic senatorial candidates for many years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETruman_Library1965_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETruman_Library1965-239">[236]</a></sup> Upon turning 80 in 1964, Truman was feted in Washington, and addressed the Senate, availing himself of a new rule that allowed former presidents to be granted <a href="/wiki/Floor_privileges_of_the_United_States_Senate" class="mw-redirect" title="Floor privileges of the United States Senate">privilege of the floor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992983_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992983-240">[237]</a></sup> After a fall in his home in late 1964, his physical condition declined. In 1965, President <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> signed the <a href="/wiki/Medicare_(United_States)" title="Medicare (United States)">Medicare</a> bill at the <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman_Presidential_Library_and_Museum" title="Harry S. Truman Presidential Library and Museum">Harry S. Truman Presidential Library and Museum</a> and gave the first two Medicare cards to Truman and his wife Bess to honor the former president's fight for government health care while in office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETruman_Library1965_239-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETruman_Library1965-239">[236]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harry_S._Truman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TrumanFuneralWreath.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/TrumanFuneralWreath.jpg/220px-TrumanFuneralWreath.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/TrumanFuneralWreath.jpg/330px-TrumanFuneralWreath.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6f/TrumanFuneralWreath.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="264" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TrumanFuneralWreath.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Wreath" title="Wreath">Wreath</a> at Truman's casket on the day of his funeral, December 27, 1972, <a href="/wiki/Independence,_Missouri" title="Independence, Missouri">Independence, Missouri</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On December 5, 1972, Truman was admitted to Kansas City's <a href="/wiki/Research_Medical_Center" title="Research Medical Center">Research Hospital and Medical Center</a> with lung congestion from <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>. He developed multiple organ failure and died at 7:50&#160;am on December 26 at the age of 88.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETruman_Library2012i_225-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETruman_Library2012i-225">[222]</a></sup> Bess Truman opted for a simple private service at the library for her husband rather than a state funeral in Washington. A week after the funeral, foreign dignitaries and Washington officials attended a memorial service at <a href="/wiki/Washington_National_Cathedral" title="Washington National Cathedral">Washington National Cathedral</a>. Bess died in 1982; they are buried at the Harry S. Truman Library and Museum in Independence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWashington_National_Cathedral2012_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWashington_National_Cathedral2012-241">[238]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWooten19731_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWooten19731-242">[239]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tributes_and_legacy">Tributes and legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harry_S._Truman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Tributes and legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Biographer Robert Donovan has tried to capture Truman's personality:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Vigorous, hard-working, simple, he had grown up close to the soil of the Midwest and understood the struggles of the people on the farms and in the small towns....After 10 years in the Senate, he had risen above the Pendergast organization. Still, he <i>had</i> come from a world of two-bit politicians, and its aura was one that he never was able to shed entirely. And he <i>did</i> retain certain characteristics one often sees in machine-bred politicians: intense partisanship, stubborn loyalty, a certain insensitivity about the transgressions of political associates, and a disinclination for the companionship of intellectuals and artists.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">[240]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harry_S._Truman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Truman_pass-the-buck.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Man in suit sitting behind desk with sign that says &quot;The buck stops here&quot;" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1f/Truman_pass-the-buck.jpg" width="200" height="191" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="191" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Truman_pass-the-buck.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Truman poses in 1959 at the recreation of the Truman Oval Office at the Truman Library in 1959, with the famous "<a href="/wiki/The_Buck_Stops_Here" class="mw-redirect" title="The Buck Stops Here">The Buck Stops Here</a>" sign on his desk. (The reverse of the sign says, "I'm From Missouri".)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Citing continuing divisions within the Democratic Party, the ongoing Cold War, and the <a href="/wiki/Boom_and_bust" class="mw-redirect" title="Boom and bust">boom and bust</a> cycle, Journalist Samuel Lubell in 1952 stated that "after seven years of Truman's hectic, even furious, activity the nation seemed to be about on the same general spot as when he first came to office&#160;... Nowhere in the whole Truman record can one point to a single, decisive break-through&#160;... All his skills and energies—and he was among our hardest-working Presidents—were directed to standing still".<sup id="cite_ref-lubell1956_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lubell1956-244">[241]</a></sup> When he left office in 1953, Truman was one of the most unpopular chief executives in history. His job approval rating of 22% in the Gallup Poll of February 1952 was lower than Richard Nixon's 24% in August 1974, the month that Nixon resigned.</p>
<p>American public feeling towards Truman grew steadily warmer with the passing years; as early as 1962, a poll of 75 historians conducted by <a href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Schlesinger,_Sr." class="mw-redirect" title="Arthur M. Schlesinger, Sr.">Arthur M. Schlesinger, Sr.</a> ranked Truman among the "near great" presidents. The period following his death consolidated a partial rehabilitation of his legacy among both historians and members of the public.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27Wisconsin_Magazine_of_History.27.27Autumn_1975_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27Wisconsin_Magazine_of_History.27.27Autumn_1975-245">[242]</a></sup> Truman died when the nation was consumed with crises in <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Watergate_scandal" title="Watergate scandal">Watergate</a>, and his death brought a new wave of attention to his political career.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27Time.27.27December_3.2C_1973_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27Time.27.27December_3.2C_1973-177">[174]</a></sup> In the early and mid-1970s, Truman captured the popular imagination much as he had in 1948, this time emerging as a kind of political folk hero, a president who was thought to exemplify an integrity and accountability many observers felt was lacking in <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Richard_Nixon" title="Presidency of Richard Nixon">the Nixon White House</a>. This public reassessment of Truman was aided by the popularity of a book of reminiscences which Truman had told to journalist <a href="/wiki/Merle_Miller" title="Merle Miller">Merle Miller</a> beginning in 1961, with the agreement that they would not be published until after Truman's death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek2008149.2C_152_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek2008149.2C_152-246">[243]</a></sup></p>
<p>Truman has had his latter-day critics as well. After a review of information available to Truman about the presence of espionage activities in the U.S. government, Democratic Senator <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Patrick_Moynihan" title="Daniel Patrick Moynihan">Daniel Patrick Moynihan</a> concluded that Truman was "almost willfully obtuse" concerning the danger of American communism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoynihan1997_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoynihan1997-247">[244]</a></sup> In 2010, historian Alonzo Hamby concluded that "Harry Truman remains a controversial president."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamby2002_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamby2002-248">[245]</a></sup> However, since leaving office, Truman has fared well in <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">polls ranking the presidents</a>. He has never been listed lower than ninth, and was ranked fifth in a <i><a href="/wiki/C-SPAN" title="C-SPAN">C-SPAN</a></i> poll in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECSPAN2009_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECSPAN2009-249">[246]</a></sup></p>
<p>The fall of the Soviet Union in 1991 caused Truman advocates to claim vindication for his decisions in the postwar period. According to Truman biographer Robert Dallek, "His contribution to victory in the cold war without a devastating nuclear conflict elevated him to the stature of a great or near-great president."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek2008152_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek2008152-250">[247]</a></sup> The 1992 publication of <a href="/wiki/David_McCullough" title="David McCullough">David McCullough</a>'s favorable biography of Truman further cemented the view of Truman as a highly regarded Chief Executive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek2008152_250-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek2008152-250">[247]</a></sup> According to historian Donald R. McCoy in his book on the Truman presidency,</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Harry Truman himself gave a strong and far-from-incorrect impression of being a tough, concerned and direct leader. He was occasionally vulgar, often partisan, and usually nationalistic&#160;... On his own terms, Truman can be seen as having prevented the coming of a third world war and having preserved from Communist oppression much of what he called the free world. Yet clearly he largely failed to achieve his Wilsonian aim of securing perpetual peace, making the world safe for democracy, and advancing opportunities for individual development internationally.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1984318.E2.80.9319_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1984318.E2.80.9319-251">[248]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sites_and_honors">Sites and honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harry_S._Truman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Sites and honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harry_S_TRuman_1973_Issue-8c.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Harry_S_TRuman_1973_Issue-8c.jpg/220px-Harry_S_TRuman_1973_Issue-8c.jpg" width="220" height="345" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Harry_S_TRuman_1973_Issue-8c.jpg/330px-Harry_S_TRuman_1973_Issue-8c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/94/Harry_S_TRuman_1973_Issue-8c.jpg 2x" data-file-width="390" data-file-height="612" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Harry_S_TRuman_1973_Issue-8c.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">1973 <a href="/wiki/Presidents_of_the_United_States_on_U.S._postage_stamps#Harry_S._Truman" title="Presidents of the United States on U.S. postage stamps">stamp issued following Truman's death</a>—Truman has been honored on five U.S. postage stamps, issued between 1973 and 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKloetzelCharles201250.2C_61.2C_71.2C_91.2C_99_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKloetzelCharles201250.2C_61.2C_71.2C_91.2C_99-252">[249]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1953, Truman received the <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Bublick_Award" title="Solomon Bublick Award">Solomon Bublick Award</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_University_of_Jerusalem" title="Hebrew University of Jerusalem">Hebrew University of Jerusalem</a>. In 1956, Truman traveled to Europe with his wife. In England, he met with Churchill and received an honorary <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Civil_Law" title="Doctor of Civil Law">Doctor of Civil Law</a> degree from Oxford University. Across Britain he was hailed; London's <i>Daily Telegraph</i> characterized Truman as the "Living and kicking symbol of everything that everybody likes best about the United States."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992952.E2.80.9359.2C_quote_on_p._959_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough1992952.E2.80.9359.2C_quote_on_p._959-253">[250]</a></sup> In 1959, he was given a 50-year award by the <a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">Masons</a>, recognizing his longstanding involvement: he was initiated on February 9, 1909, into the Belton <a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">Masonic</a> Lodge in Missouri. In 1911, he helped establish the Grandview Lodge, and he served as its first Worshipful Master. In September 1940, during his Senate re-election campaign, Truman was elected <a href="/wiki/Grand_Master_(Masonic)" title="Grand Master (Masonic)">Grand Master</a> of the Missouri <a href="/wiki/Grand_Lodge" title="Grand Lodge">Grand Lodge</a> of Freemasonry; Truman said later that the Masonic election assured his victory in the general election. In 1945, he was made a 33° Sovereign Grand Inspector General and an Honorary Member of the supreme council at the Supreme Council A.A.S.R. Southern Jurisdiction Headquarters in Washington D.C.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrand_Lodge-Pennsylvania2011_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrand_Lodge-Pennsylvania2011-254">[251]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27Time.27.27March_24.2C_1952_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27Time.27.27March_24.2C_1952-255">[252]</a></sup> Truman was also a member of <a href="/wiki/Sons_of_the_American_Revolution" title="Sons of the American Revolution">Sons of the American Revolution</a> (SAR)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETruman_Library.2C_SAR2012_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETruman_Library.2C_SAR2012-256">[253]</a></sup> and a card-carrying member of the <a href="/wiki/Sons_of_Confederate_Veterans" title="Sons of Confederate Veterans">Sons of Confederate Veterans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMissouri_Partisan_Ranger1995_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMissouri_Partisan_Ranger1995-257">[254]</a></sup> Two of his relatives were <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate</a> soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMissouri_Partisan_Ranger1995_257-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMissouri_Partisan_Ranger1995-257">[254]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEakinHale199571_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEakinHale199571-258">[255]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1975, the <a href="/wiki/Truman_Scholarship" class="mw-redirect" title="Truman Scholarship">Truman Scholarship</a> was created as a federal program to honor U.S. college students who exemplified dedication to public service and leadership in public policy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETruman_Scholarship2012_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETruman_Scholarship2012-259">[256]</a></sup> In 2004, the President Harry S. Truman Fellowship in National Security Science and Engineering was created as a distinguished postdoctoral three-year appointment at <a href="/wiki/Sandia_National_Laboratories" title="Sandia National Laboratories">Sandia National Laboratories</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETruman_Fellowship2012_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETruman_Fellowship2012-260">[257]</a></sup> In 2001, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Missouri" title="University of Missouri">University of Missouri</a> established the Harry S. Truman School of Public Affairs to advance the study and practice of governance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETruman_School_of_Public_Affairs2010_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETruman_School_of_Public_Affairs2010-261">[258]</a></sup> The University of Missouri's <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Tigers" title="Missouri Tigers">Missouri Tigers</a> athletic programs have an official mascot named <a href="/wiki/Truman_the_Tiger" title="Truman the Tiger">Truman the Tiger</a>. On July 1, 1996, Northeast Missouri State University became <a href="/wiki/Truman_State_University" title="Truman State University">Truman State University</a>—to mark its transformation from a <a href="/wiki/Teachers_College" class="mw-redirect" title="Teachers College">teachers' college</a> to a highly selective <a href="/wiki/Liberal_arts_university" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberal arts university">liberal arts university</a> and to honor the only Missourian to become president. A member institution of the <a href="/wiki/City_Colleges_of_Chicago" title="City Colleges of Chicago">City Colleges of Chicago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harry_S_Truman_College" class="mw-redirect" title="Harry S Truman College">Harry S Truman College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chicago,_Illinois" class="mw-redirect" title="Chicago, Illinois">Chicago, Illinois</a>, is named in his honor for his dedication to public colleges and universities. In 2000, the headquarters for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">State Department</a>, built in the 1930s but never officially named, was dedicated as the <a href="/wiki/Harry_S_Truman_Building" title="Harry S Truman Building">Harry S Truman Building</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECNN2000_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECNN2000-262">[259]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite Truman's attempt to curtail the naval carrier arm, which led to the 1949 <a href="/wiki/Revolt_of_the_Admirals" title="Revolt of the Admirals">Revolt of the Admirals</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27Time.27.27October_17.2C_1949_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27Time.27.27October_17.2C_1949-263">[260]</a></sup> an aircraft carrier is named after him. The <a href="/wiki/USS_Harry_S._Truman_(CVN-75)" class="mw-redirect" title="USS Harry S. Truman (CVN-75)">USS&#160;<i>Harry S. Truman</i>&#160;(CVN-75)</a> was christened on September 7, 1996. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENavSource_Online2012_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENavSource_Online2012-264">[261]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/129th_Field_Artillery_Regiment_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="129th Field Artillery Regiment (United States)">129th Field Artillery Regiment</a> is designated "Truman's Own" in recognition of Truman's service as commander of its D Battery during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmy_National_Guard2012_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmy_National_Guard2012-265">[262]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1984, Truman was posthumously awarded the United States <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Gold_Medal" title="Congressional Gold Medal">Congressional Gold Medal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">[263]</a></sup> In 1991, he was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Hall_of_Famous_Missourians" title="Hall of Famous Missourians">Hall of Famous Missourians</a>, and a bronze bust depicting him is on permanent display in the rotunda of the <a href="/wiki/Missouri_State_Capitol" title="Missouri State Capitol">Missouri State Capitol</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27Hall_of_Famous_Missourians.27.272012_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27Hall_of_Famous_Missourians.27.272012-267">[264]</a></sup> Other sites associated with Truman include:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman_National_Historic_Site" title="Harry S. Truman National Historic Site">Harry S. Truman National Historic Site</a> includes the Wallace House at 219 N. Delaware in Independence and the family farmhouse at <a href="/wiki/Grandview,_Missouri" title="Grandview, Missouri">Grandview, Missouri</a> (Truman sold most of the farm for Kansas City suburban development including the Truman Corners Shopping Center).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Harry_S_Truman_Birthplace_State_Historic_Site" title="Harry S Truman Birthplace State Historic Site">Harry S Truman Birthplace State Historic Site</a> is the house where Truman was born and spent 11 months in Lamar, Missouri.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETruman_Birthplace2012_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETruman_Birthplace2012-268">[265]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman_Presidential_Library_and_Museum" title="Harry S. Truman Presidential Library and Museum">Harry S. Truman Presidential Library and Museum</a>&#160;– The <a href="/wiki/Presidential_library" title="Presidential library">Presidential library</a> in Independence</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman_Little_White_House" title="Harry S. Truman Little White House">Harry S. Truman Little White House</a>&#160;– Truman's winter getaway at <a href="/wiki/Key_West,_Florida" title="Key West, Florida">Key West, Florida</a></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harry_S._Truman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">
<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">

</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harry_S._Truman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Truman was Vice President under President <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> and became President upon Roosevelt's death on April 12, 1945. As this was prior to the adoption of the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-fifth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-fifth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Twenty-Fifth Amendment</a> in 1967, a vacancy in the office of Vice President was not filled.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-initialS-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-initialS_4-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-initialS_4-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Truman was not given a middle name, only the initial S. There is controversy over whether this initial should be followed by a period (or <i><a href="/wiki/Full_stop" title="Full stop">full stop</a></i>), although Truman's own archived correspondence suggests that he regularly used the period when writing his name.<sup id="cite_ref-LibraryPeriod_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LibraryPeriod-3">[2]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-111"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-111">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example, see <cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Paul_Fussell" title="Paul Fussell">Fussell, Paul</a> (1988). "Thank God for the Atomic Bomb". <i>Thank God for the Atomic Bomb and Other Essays</i>. New York Summit Books.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHarry+S.+Truman&amp;rft.atitle=Thank+God+for+the+Atomic+Bomb&amp;rft.aufirst=Paul&amp;rft.aulast=Fussell&amp;rft.btitle=Thank+God+for+the+Atomic+Bomb+and+Other+Essays&amp;rft.date=1988&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.pub=New+York+Summit+Books&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton2009301-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton2009301_79-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFHamilton2009">Hamilton 2009</a>, p.&#160;301: "Over seven years (1941–1948) the committee heard from 1,798 witnesses during 432 public hearings. It published nearly two thousand pages of documents and saved perhaps $15&#160;billion and thousands of lives by exposing faulty airplane and munitions production."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-87"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-87">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Harold_Foote_Gosnell" title="Harold Foote Gosnell">Harold Foote Gosnell</a>, <i>Truman's Crises: A Political Biography of Harry S. Truman</i> (Greenwood Press, 1980), p. 212: "On only one occasion did [Truman] break a tie, and this was when his negative vote defeated a Taft amendment to the Lend-Lease Act which would have prevented postwar delivery of lend-lease goods contracted for during the war."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-88"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-88">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Robert_C._Byrd" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert C. Byrd">Robert C. Byrd</a>, <i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=VL4QcQR-W6YC&amp;pg=PA534&amp;lpg=PA534">Senate, 1789-1989, Vol. 1: Addresses on the History of the United States Senate</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Government_Printing_Office" class="mw-redirect" title="Government Printing Office">Government Printing Office</a>, 1988), p. 534: "In his eighty-two days as vice president, he had the opportunity to vote only once--on an amendment to limit the Lend-Lease extension bill. The vote was tied, and Truman voted no, which, in a sense, was unnecessary since the bill would have died even without his vote."</span></li>
